  • 3. Open this page: explain the dashboard before drilling into details.
  • 4. Show audit logic: GREEN is direct support, YELLOW is partial/inferred, RED is failure or contradiction.

1. Run Summary

This section is the executive checksum for the run. It records the run ID, verifies the most important ordering constraint, and defines the report's audit colors. The key process guarantee is that literature search must occur before hypothesis generation, so the generated idea is grounded in retrieved papers rather than produced from the input PDF alone. Read GREEN/YELLOW/RED as claim-audit labels, not as paper-quality labels.

Run ID: fdb5be77-5022-4055-b94f-71f9e43a61bb

Literature search happened before hypothesis generation: YES.

GREEN labels require verified references, bibliographic score >= 0.75, direct support, evidence snippets, provenance, and a written reason. The score column in the verification table is bibliographic identity confidence, not support strength. YELLOW means uncertain, partial, or a novel claim that still needs experiment; RED means not found, unsupported, contradicted, or clearly wrong.

Direct evidenceReference identity is verified, evidence explicitly addresses the claim, and the reason explains the support relation.
Indirect evidenceEvidence is relevant background or motivation, but it does not directly prove the full claim.
Background / absentEvidence is too weak, abstract-only, missing, or only useful as context.
Contradictory evidenceEvidence conflicts with the claim or contains explicit contradiction cues.

Four Error Categories

Unsupported ClaimThe conclusion is not supported by any available input material.
OverclaimThe material supports a weaker conclusion, but the generated claim states a stronger one.
Mis-citationThe cited material is topic-related but does not support the specific claim.
ContradictionThe generated claim conflicts with the cited material.

2. Input Paper Profiles

This section shows the structured profile extracted from each submitted PDF before any external retrieval or hypothesis generation. A profile contains the detected title, authors, abstract, and high-level source claims. These fields are produced by deterministic PDF parsing and profiling heuristics, with optional LLM assistance only when enabled. They are not treated as final truth: if the title, abstract, author list, or key claims are wrong, later search queries, relatedness filtering, literature-map items, and citation audits should be interpreted cautiously.

Clean profileTitle, authors, abstract, and key claims match the PDF; downstream retrieval is likely aligned.
Partial profileSome fields are missing or noisy; search may still work, but map and hypothesis quality should be reviewed manually.
Bad profileTitle/abstract/key claims are visibly wrong; later sections may be answering the wrong paper and should be treated as unreliable.

ORGEval: Graph-Theoretic Evaluation of LLMs in Optimization Modeling

Authors: Zhuohan Wang, Ziwei Zhu, Ziniu Li, Congliang Chen, Yizhou Han, Yufeng Lin, et al.

Abstract: Formulating optimization problems for industrial applications demands significant manual effort and domain expertise. While Large Language Models (LLMs) show promise in automating this process, evaluating their performance remains difficult due to the absence of robust metrics. Existing solver-based approaches often face inconsistency, infeasibility issues, and high computational costs. To address these issues, we propose ORGEval, a graph-theoretic evaluation framework for assessing LLMs’ capabilities in formulating linear and mixed-integer linear programs. ORGEval represents optimization models as graphs, reducing equivalence detection to graph isomorphism testing. We identify and prove a sufficient condition, when the tested graphs are symmetric decomposable (SD), under which the Weisfeiler–Lehman (WL) test is guaranteed to correctly detect isomorphism. Building on this, ORGEval integrates a tailored variant of the WL-test with an SD detection algorithm to evaluate model equivalence. By focusing on structural equivalence rather than instance-level configurations, ORGEval is robust to numerical variations. Experimental results show that our method can successfully detect model equivalence and produce 100% consistent results across random parameter configurations, while significantly outperforming solver-based methods in runtime, especially on difficult problems. Leveraging ORGEval, we construct the Bench4Opt dataset and benchmark state-of-the-art LLMs on optimization modeling. Our results reveal that although optimization modeling remains challenging for all LLMs, DeepSeek-V3 and Claude-Opus-4 achieve the highest accuracies under direct prompting, outperforming even leading reasoning models.

Key claims:

  • 4.2 Advantages of ORGEval Evaluation Efficiency Empirically, we demonstrate that ORGEval offers significantly higher evaluation efficiency compared with solver-based methods, especially for problem instances that are challenging for solvers.
  • To address these issues, we propose ORGEval, a graph-theoretic evaluation framework for assessing LLMs’ capabilities in formulating linear and mixed-integer linear programs.
  • Our experiments demonstrate that ORGEval achieves consistent evaluation results across all data configurations and offers significant speed advantages over solver-based methods, particularly on hard-to-solve problems.
  • Experimental results show that our method can successfully detect model equivalence and produce 100% consistent results across random parameter configurations, while significantly outperforming solver-based methods in runtime, especially on difficult problems.

3. Paper Index Legend

This section defines the shorthand paper labels used throughout the report. Labels such as P1, P2, ... refer to retrieved or connected papers after deduplication and relatedness filtering; the internal JSON index remains zero-based, so P1 corresponds to JSON index 0. The relation field explains why a paper entered the candidate pool, for example input seed, cited by the input paper, citing the input paper, semantic expansion, or topical search. The relatedness score is the gate score used before map and hypothesis generation; it is not a citation-support score.

Distance 0 / input seedThe submitted paper itself; useful as context but not enough to prove a new idea.
Distance 1-2Cited-by or citing-neighborhood papers; these are stronger candidates for connected literature.
Semantic expansionTitle/abstract-near papers added when citation graph is sparse; useful but weaker than citation-chain evidence.
Low relatednessRejected or marginal papers should not support hypotheses, even if they are real papers.

4. Literature Map Summary

This section turns the connected paper pool into a literature map. A thread is a cluster of papers that share a method, problem setting, or conceptual role. A gap is a missing test, boundary condition, mechanism, or application regime suggested by the connected evidence. A tension records an unresolved contrast, such as two papers making claims that are hard to compare directly or using incompatible assumptions. A cross-domain connection is a possible transfer of an idea from one setting to another. These items are generated from paper titles, abstracts, retrieved snippets, and evidence IDs; they are the immediate inputs to hypothesis generation, not final claims by themselves.

Thread onlyThe literature clusters cleanly, but may not yet imply a new research question.
Gap + evidenceA plausible place to generate a hypothesis; check evidence IDs before trusting it.
TensionA stronger idea source when assumptions, benchmarks, or claims do not line up across papers.
Empty tension/connectionNot a failure by itself; it means the retrieved literature did not expose a clear contrast or transfer opportunity.

Map generation mode: llm. Fallback used: False. Reason: LLM returned a valid literature map with at least one thread and one gap.

Threads

  • T1 Graph-theoretic evaluation of LLM-generated optimization models: ORGEval (P1, P2) introduces a graph-theoretic framework that represents LP/MILP formulations as bipartite graphs and reduces equivalence checking to graph isomorphism, using symmetric decomposability (SD) as a sufficient condition for the Weisfeiler-Lehman test to be exact. This provides a provably correct, efficient alternative to solver-based evaluation.
    Evidence: Evidence 4 Evidence 5 Evidence 6 Evidence 12 Evidence 14 Evidence 15 Evidence 16 Evidence 17
  • T2 Data synthesis and fine-tuning for optimization modeling: Several works (P3, P5, P7) propose data synthesis methods (ReSocratic, OR-Instruct) to generate high-quality training data for LLMs in optimization modeling, aiming to bridge the gap between small open-source and large closed-source models.
    Evidence: Evidence 21 Evidence 26 Evidence 25 Evidence 34 Evidence 35 Evidence 37
  • T3 Practical deployment and scalability of LLM-based optimization assistants: Optimus (P4) discusses challenges in deploying LLMs for real-world optimization, including ambiguous problem descriptions, high cost of large models, and the need for user feedback integration. It suggests that moderate-accuracy AI can still augment human experts in non-safety-critical domains.
    Evidence: Evidence 27 Evidence 29 Evidence 30 Evidence 32

Gaps

  • G1: No empirical comparison of ORGEval's graph-theoretic equivalence detection with other equivalence checking methods (e.g., Equivamap) on a common benchmark. Why it matters: Without comparative evaluation, it is unclear whether ORGEval's SD-based approach is more accurate or efficient than alternative LLM-based equivalence checkers, hindering adoption.
    Evidence: Evidence 4 Evidence 15 Evidence 39
  • G2: The SD condition's applicability to nonlinear programming (NLP) or mixed-integer nonlinear programming (MINLP) formulations is not addressed. Why it matters: Many real-world optimization problems involve nonlinearities; extending ORGEval to NLP/MINLP would broaden its impact.
    Evidence: Evidence 6 Evidence 15
  • G3: No study investigates whether LLMs can learn to generate SD-certified formulations directly, reducing the need for post-hoc equivalence checking. Why it matters: If LLMs can be trained to produce SD formulations, evaluation becomes simpler and more reliable, potentially improving overall modeling accuracy.
    Evidence: Evidence 4 Evidence 17 Evidence 21 Evidence 34
  • G4: The impact of ambiguous natural language descriptions on LLM formulation accuracy is not quantified in the connected papers. Why it matters: Ambiguity is a key challenge in real-world deployment; understanding its effect can guide prompt engineering and data collection.
    Evidence: Evidence 29

Tensions

  • X1: ORGEval claims 100% consistency across data configurations (P1, P2), but solver-based methods are inconsistent and often infeasible. However, solver-based methods directly test feasibility and optimality, which are the ultimate practical concerns. There is a tension between structural equivalence (graph isomorphism) and functional equivalence (same optimal value/solution).
    Evidence: Evidence 12 Evidence 15
  • X2: P3 (OptiBench) focuses on end-to-end problem solving with numerical answers, while P1/P2 focus on formulation equivalence without solving. This reflects a tension between evaluating the entire pipeline (formulation + solving) versus evaluating only the formulation step.
    Evidence: Evidence 24 Evidence 4
  • X3: P4 (Optimus) suggests that moderate-accuracy AI can still be useful as an assistant, while P1/P2 show that even the best LLMs achieve only ~55% accuracy on Bench4Opt. There is a tension between the practical utility of imperfect models and the high accuracy required for fully automated optimization.
    Evidence: Evidence 32 Evidence 14

Cross-Domain Connections

  • C1: Graph isomorphism testing from theoretical computer science is applied to evaluate optimization model equivalence, enabling provably correct evaluation. Rationale: The WL test, originally developed for graph isomorphism, is adapted to bipartite graphs representing LP/MILP formulations, with SD condition ensuring exactness.
    Evidence: Evidence 5 Evidence 6
  • C2: Socratic questioning and back-translation from education and NLP are used to synthesize training data for optimization modeling, improving LLM performance. Rationale: ReSocratic reverses the typical question-answer synthesis: it first builds a structured optimization demonstration step-by-step, then generates a natural language question from it, leveraging pedagogical structuring.
    Evidence: Evidence 26

5. Selected Hypothesis Portfolio

This section is the short answer: it presents the selected research-idea portfolio. By default the report keeps up to two hypotheses: a primary proposal and, when available, a distinct secondary or more incremental proposal. Each hypothesis is generated from the literature map and connected evidence, then scored for literature support, novelty, feasibility, cross-domain value, risk, obvious-extension penalty, and prior-work similarity. The primary hypothesis is used for novelty search and citation audit below. The secondary hypothesis is retained as a usable alternative, not as proof that it is equally novel or equally well supported.

Primary + high noveltyBest candidate for a new research direction, subject to novelty-search caveats.
Primary + high riskInteresting but fragile; prioritize feasibility and falsification before presenting it as a project.
Incremental secondaryUsable fallback or extension; expected to be less novel but often easier to test.
No evidence IDsIdea is mostly model inference; it should not be treated as source-supported.

Hypothesis generation mode: llm. Fallback used: False. Reason: LLM returned valid hypothesis candidates after binding and review.
Review: {'used': True, 'reason': 'LLM review returned replacement/refined hypotheses.', 'candidate_count': 2}

primary

H1 - ORGEval's structural equivalence does not correlate perfectly with functional equivalence (same optimal value) for MILP formulations; there exist structurally equivalent models that yield different optimal values under different data configurations.

Total score: 0.575 Inference: inferred Novelty: 0.8 Feasibility: 0.9 Obvious-extension penalty: 0.1

Why retained: Primary proposal selected for the main novelty search and citation audit. It is the strongest non-incremental candidate when available; otherwise it is the highest-scoring distinct candidate.

Motivation: Tension X1: ORGEval claims 100% consistency across data configurations, but functional equivalence is the practical goal. If structural equivalence does not imply functional equivalence, ORGEval may miss practically important differences.

Novelty position: Directly challenges the practical relevance of ORGEval's metric; if true, would motivate hybrid evaluation combining structural and solver-based checks.

How to test it: Select a set of structurally equivalent model pairs from Bench4Opt (as determined by ORGEval). For each pair, generate random data configurations and solve both models with a solver. Record optimal values and compare. Measure proportion of pairs where optimal values differ.

Premise evidence: Evidence 12 Evidence 8

Supporting papers: P1: ORGEval: Graph-Theoretic Evaluation of LLMs in Optimization Modeling P2: Certified Evaluation for LLMs in Optimization Modeling: From Graph Isomorphism to Formulation Isomorphism

Risks:

  • ORGEval's structural equivalence may already imply functional equivalence for LP/MILP; negative result would weaken the hypothesis.
incremental

H2 - LLMs fine-tuned on SD-certified formulations (e.g., using ReSocratic-style synthesis) will produce more structurally correct models than those fine-tuned on general optimization data, as measured by ORGEval.

Total score: 0.39 Inference: inferred Novelty: 0.6 Feasibility: 0.7 Obvious-extension penalty: 0.3

Why retained: Retained as a more conservative A+B, transfer, or implementation-adjacent direction that is distinct from the primary proposal. It may be useful follow-up work, but it should not be read as the most novel hypothesis.

Motivation: G3 suggests training LLMs to produce SD-certified formulations could simplify evaluation. ReSocratic (P3) shows data synthesis improves performance. Combining these ideas may yield better models.

Novelty position: Novel combination of SD certification with data synthesis; prior work (P3) did not use SD condition.

How to test it: Construct a training set of SD-certified formulation pairs from Bench4Opt. Fine-tune Llama-3-8b using ReSocratic-style back-translation. Evaluate on Bench4Opt test set using ORGEval accuracy. Compare to baseline fine-tuned on non-SD data.

Premise evidence: Evidence 21 Evidence 25 Evidence 26

Supporting papers: P1: ORGEval: Graph-Theoretic Evaluation of LLMs in Optimization Modeling P2: Certified Evaluation for LLMs in Optimization Modeling: From Graph Isomorphism to Formulation Isomorphism P3: Benchmarking LLMs for Optimization Modeling and Enhancing Reasoning via Reverse Socratic Synthesis

Risks:

  • SD-certified data may be limited; fine-tuning may not generalize to non-SD problems.

6. Hypothesis Quality Audit

This section audits the generated idea itself, not just the references attached to it. The hypothesis is decomposed into components such as idea core, motivation, novelty position, mechanism, feasibility, experiment, and risks. Each component receives a small relevance-ranked evidence bundle when directly relevant evidence exists; otherwise it is labeled as model-inferred or needing review. This avoids padding the hypothesis with weak citations while still showing which parts are grounded in evidence IDs.

Source-stated + GREENThe component restates source text with provenance; it is evidence, not novelty.
Evidence-inspired + YELLOWThe component is motivated by evidence but goes beyond what papers directly prove.
Model-inferred + YELLOWThe component is a generated bridge or mechanism; it needs human review and experiments.
Unsupported / REDThe component lacks evidence, contradicts evidence, or makes an unsupported strong claim.

Summary: Hypothesis quality audit separates what the literature directly says from what the model inferred as a new idea. Generated idea, novelty, and experiment components should usually be YELLOW unless directly restating an established finding.

Components: 14; by status: {'evidence_inspired': 10, 'model_inferred': 1, 'needs_human_review': 3}; by color: {'yellow': 14}; precision evidence per component: None

We study this issue in automated optimization modeling, where LLMs translate natural-language descriptions and numerical data into solver-ready formulations. Existing solver-based evaluation only approximates modeling correctness: it offers no formulation-level guarantee, is uninformative for infeasible cases, and can be expensive on hard mixed-integer linear programming. To address these limitations, We propose ORGEval, a graph-theoretic evaluation framework that represents optimization formulations as bipartite graphs and reduces equivalence checking to graph isomorphism.

Finally, empirical results obtained through this benchmark reveal concrete capability limits and failure modes of current LLMs in optimization modeling. Evidence 17literature map T1, literature map G3Certified Evaluation for LLMs in Optimization Modeling: From Graph Isomorphism to Formulation Isomorphismpdf:conclusion:p8
literature map T1: context
literature map G3: context
All groundtruth instances in Bench4Opt are verified to satisfy the SD condition, making the benchmark a certified evaluation setting rather than only a collection of tasks and reference answers. Experiments show that ORGEval achieves 100% consistency across data configurations and evaluates hard MIPLIB instances within seconds, while solver-based evaluation can become unstable, uninformative, or computationally prohibitive. Benchmarking state-of-the-art LLMs on Bench4Opt reveals that optimization modeling remains difficult: the best models achieve only 54.82% pass@1 accuracy, and error analysis shows that constraint specification and decision-variable design are the dominant failure modes. ORGEval connects theory, benchmark design, and empirical analysis: graph isomorphism and SD provide a reliable evaluation oracle, Bench4Opt instantiates it with SD-certified examples, and the results expose current LLM limitations. This highlights a broader principle for structured-domain benchmarks: evaluation should specify not only model scores, but also when the oracle is provably reliable.

While Large Language Models (LLMs) show promise in automating this process, evaluating their performance remains difficult due to the absence of robust metrics. To address these issues, we propose ORGEval, a graph-theoretic evaluation framework for assessing LLMs’ capabilities in formulating linear and mixed-integer linear programs. We identify and prove a sufficient condition, when the tested graphs are symmetric decomposable (SD), under which the Weisfeiler–Lehman (WL) test is guaranteed to correctly detect isomorphism. Experimental results show that our method can successfully detect model equivalence and produce 100% consistent results across random parameter configurations, while significantly outperforming solver-based methods in runtime, especially on difficult problems. Our results reveal that although optimization modeling remains challenging for all LLMs, DeepSeek-V3 and Claude-Opus-4 achieve the highest accuracies under direct prompting, outperforming even leading reasoning models.

To address these limitations, we propose ORGEval, a graph-theoretic evaluation framework for assessing LLMs’ capabilities in formulating linear and mixed-integer linear programs (MILPs). To make model-level equivalence evaluation tractable, we reduce model "equivalence" to model isomorphism, a stricter form of equivalence that focuses on the inherent structure of models rather than their optimal solutions. Importantly, we observe that if two instances are isomorphic, their isomorphism remains unchanged under different data configurations. ORGEval is developed to evaluate modeling equivalence by detecting whether the inherent structure of two random instances from two models is equivalent, thus making the evaluation stable when altering instance parameters.

2.3 Model Isomorphism As an alternative, we further define model isomorphism to capture structural equivalence between models, rather than relying on numerical optimal values. We say two optimization models M1 and M2 are model isomorphic if the following conditions hold: • Shared parameter support: M1 and M2 share the same parameter support Θ; • Existence of permutation-invariant transformation: There exists a permutation mapping F1 over decision variables and F2 over constraints such that, for any θ ∈Θ, the transformed instance F1 ◦F2(M1(θ)) is exactly M2(θ). Note that model isomorphism is sufficient for mutual reducibility: if M1 and M2 are isomorphic, then M1 and M2 are mutually model-losslessreducible. Optimization Model Equivalence Previous work typically assesses instance-level equivalence by comparing the optimal values of P1 = M1(θ) and P2 = M2(θ) for specific θ, where both the model and data are coupled in the input prompt. Different from previous work, we aim to detect model-level equivalence and consider a model-data separated framework for evaluating autonomous modeling systems.
Evidence 58CL3 supportCertified Evaluation for LLMs in Optimization Modeling: From Graph Isomorphism to Formulation Isomorphismpdf:abstract:p1
CL3 support: indirect
Uncertain or partial support: Available evidence motivates this generated claim, but it cannot directly prove the full claim.
To make this practical and reliable, we introduce symmetric decomposability (SD), a checkable condition under which Weisfeiler-Lehman color equivalence is exactly equivalent to formulation isomorphism. We further constructed Bench4Opt, a benchmark that separates models from data and contains SD-certified ground-truth instances. Experiments show that ORGEval reliably detects formulation equivalence while being substantially faster than solver-based evaluation, especially on challenging instances.
Evidence 59CL3 supportCertified Evaluation for LLMs in Optimization Modeling: From Graph Isomorphism to Formulation Isomorphismpdf:introduction:p2
CL3 support: indirect
Uncertain or partial support: Available evidence motivates this generated claim, but it cannot directly prove the full claim.
Therefore, evaluating optimization modeling requires an equivalence notion that is invariant to superficial re-indexing but sensitive to structural modeling errors. Existing OR modeling benchmarks commonly rely on solver-based evaluation: the generated and reference formulations are solved under a fixed data configuration, and their optimal objective values are compared (Tang et al., 2024; Huang et al., 2024; AhmadiTeshnizi et al., 2024; Yang et al., 2024b). Non-equivalent models can coincidentally share the same optimal value; infeasible instances make objective-value comparison uninformative; repeated solver calls can be costly for large or difficult mixed interger programming (MILPs).

7. Hypothesis Novelty Search

This section searches for close prior work after the selected hypothesis is generated. After the primary hypothesis is selected, the system creates novelty-search queries from the hypothesis text, method terms, target problem, and supporting-paper neighborhood, then searches external literature again. The result is a risk assessment: exact/close matches suggest the idea may already exist, near matches suggest the idea needs sharper differentiation, related works provide background, and missing results only mean no close prior work was found by available tools. This section never proves novelty conclusively.

High riskExact or close prior work found; the idea likely needs substantial differentiation.
Medium riskNear work shares method, target problem, or framing; novelty may survive only with a sharper mechanism.
Low riskOnly background-related work found; still not proof of novelty.
Unknown riskSearch failed or evidence is too sparse; do not claim novelty.

Novelty risk: LOW

Conclusion: No close prior work was found by available tools, but related background work exists.

Reason: 3 related work(s) were found; absence of a close match is not proof of novelty.

8. Cited Reference Integrity Audit

This section audits only the references actually cited by the selected hypothesis atomic claims. It answers a bibliographic question: does the cited reference appear to exist, and do title, authors, year, DOI, venue, or source URL point to the same paper? It deliberately does not judge whether the reference supports the scientific claim; that is handled in the next section. Field status values separate matched/present metadata from missing or uncertain metadata. Missing DOI/year usually means the field was unavailable or not comparable, not automatically that the reference is wrong.

Score is bibliographic identity confidence, not a metadata-completeness percentage. A searched bibliographic record or local input PDF can score highly even when DOI or year is missing, but non-seed references with both DOI and year missing are downgraded to YELLOW.

GREEN integrityReference identity is verified enough for audit use; claim support is still judged separately.
YELLOW integrityReference probably exists but metadata is incomplete or uncertain; support cannot be upgraded to strong confidence.
RED integrityReference is not found or metadata conflicts strongly; related claim-support rows should be RED.
Missing DOI/yearUsually source coverage limitation, not a contradiction, unless other metadata also conflicts.

9. Claim Support Audit

This section audits whether each cited reference supports the specific atomic claim attached to it. Rows in this table are claim-reference pairs, so the same claim ID may appear more than once if multiple references are checked separately. This is stricter than bibliographic integrity: a real paper can be GREEN in the previous section but YELLOW or RED here if it only provides background, supports a weaker claim, discusses a different topic, or lacks usable evidence snippets.

Support type rule: direct means verified reference + explicit evidence for the specific claim; indirect means topic-relevant evidence that motivates but does not prove the claim; background means weak/abstract-only context; absent means no usable evidence or no valid reference.

Error category rule: no_specific_error_detected means this row was not classified as unsupported claim, overclaim, mis-citation, or contradiction. It does not mean the claim is proven; use Color, Support, Support Type, Evidence IDs, and Reason for the final interpretation.

GREEN + directVerified reference and explicit evidence directly support this exact claim.
YELLOW + indirect/backgroundEvidence motivates or contextualizes the claim but does not prove it.
RED + unsupported/absentNo usable evidence, invalid reference, contradiction, or clear unsupported claim.
no_specific_error_detectedNo four-category error was detected; this can still be YELLOW if support is indirect.

10. Evidence Cards

Evidence Cards expand evidence IDs into the human-readable form used throughout the report. Each card shows the source paper, URL or path, location, snippet text, and every claim or map item that reused the evidence. The same snippet can support multiple claims in different ways, so each use has its own support type, error category, and reason. Internal hash IDs are kept only in Appendix B for reproducibility; the numbered Evidence labels are intended for review.

One evidence, one useSimple case: one snippet is used for one claim or map item.
One evidence, multiple usesEach use has its own support type and reason; do not infer one global label for the snippet.
Abstract locationUseful but shallow; direct support from abstract alone should be interpreted cautiously.
PDF/full-text locationUsually stronger provenance because the snippet comes from a section or page-level source.

We study this issue in automated optimization modeling, where LLMs translate natural-language descriptions and numerical data into solver-ready formulations. Existing solver-based evaluation only approximates modeling correctness: it offers no formulation-level guarantee, is uninformative for infeasible cases, and can be expensive on hard mixed-integer linear programming. To address these limitations, We propose ORGEval, a graph-theoretic evaluation framework that represents optimization formulations as bipartite graphs and reduces equivalence checking to graph isomorphism.

Source paper: Certified Evaluation for LLMs in Optimization Modeling: From Graph Isomorphism to Formulation Isomorphism

Finally, empirical results obtained through this benchmark reveal concrete capability limits and failure modes of current LLMs in optimization modeling. In our experiments, ORGEval achieves consistent structural evaluation across data configurations and is substantially faster than solver-based evaluation on difficult instances. When applied to state-of-the-art LLMs, Bench4Opt shows that automated optimization modeling remains challenging, and error analysis indicates that constraint specification and decision-variable design remain dominant failure modes. • We formalize optimization-modeling correctness as formulation isomorphism and reduce LP/MILP equivalence evaluation to graph isomorphism (Section 3.1 and Section 3.3).

Evidence 17

Source paper: Certified Evaluation for LLMs in Optimization Modeling: From Graph Isomorphism to Formulation Isomorphism

Source URL / path: https://openreview.net/pdf/df5fb1827fe255e561be674768a731f4a455674d.pdf

Location: pdf:conclusion:p8; Used by: literature map T1, literature map G3

Used bySupport typeError categoryReason
literature map T1contextno_specific_error_detected
literature map G3contextno_specific_error_detected
All groundtruth instances in Bench4Opt are verified to satisfy the SD condition, making the benchmark a certified evaluation setting rather than only a collection of tasks and reference answers. Experiments show that ORGEval achieves 100% consistency across data configurations and evaluates hard MIPLIB instances within seconds, while solver-based evaluation can become unstable, uninformative, or computationally prohibitive. Benchmarking state-of-the-art LLMs on Bench4Opt reveals that optimization modeling remains difficult: the best models achieve only 54.82% pass@1 accuracy, and error analysis shows that constraint specification and decision-variable design are the dominant failure modes. ORGEval connects theory, benchmark design, and empirical analysis: graph isomorphism and SD provide a reliable evaluation oracle, Bench4Opt instantiates it with SD-certified examples, and the results expose current LLM limitations. This highlights a broader principle for structured-domain benchmarks: evaluation should specify not only model scores, but also when the oracle is provably reliable.

Concluding Remarks In this paper, we propose a new path for the training of open-source large language models (LLMs) specifically tailored for optimization modeling. We characterize four critical requirements essential for the training dataset of optimization modeling LLMs and develop OR-Instruct, a semi-automated framework for generating synthetic data that meets these specific needs. We utilize the OR-Instruct data to train open-source LLMs with approximately 7 billion parameters, which significantly enhances their optimization modeling capabilities, achieving competitive performance across all test datasets.

Large language model (LLM) benchmarks typically evaluate correctness through task-specific rules, but for structured outputs, correctness is often an equivalence problem. We study this issue in automated optimization modeling, where LLMs translate natural-language descriptions and numerical data into solver-ready formulations. Existing solver-based evaluation only approximates modeling correctness: it offers no formulation-level guarantee, is uninformative for infeasible cases, and can be expensive on hard mixed-integer linear programming.

While Large Language Models (LLMs) show promise in automating this process, evaluating their performance remains difficult due to the absence of robust metrics. To address these issues, we propose ORGEval, a graph-theoretic evaluation framework for assessing LLMs’ capabilities in formulating linear and mixed-integer linear programs. We identify and prove a sufficient condition, when the tested graphs are symmetric decomposable (SD), under which the Weisfeiler–Lehman (WL) test is guaranteed to correctly detect isomorphism. Experimental results show that our method can successfully detect model equivalence and produce 100% consistent results across random parameter configurations, while significantly outperforming solver-based methods in runtime, especially on difficult problems. Our results reveal that although optimization modeling remains challenging for all LLMs, DeepSeek-V3 and Claude-Opus-4 achieve the highest accuracies under direct prompting, outperforming even leading reasoning models.

To make model-level equivalence evaluation tractable, we reduce model "equivalence" to model isomorphism, a stricter form of equivalence that focuses on the inherent structure of models rather than their optimal solutions. Importantly, we observe that if two instances are isomorphic, their isomorphism remains unchanged under different data configurations. ORGEval is developed to evaluate modeling equivalence by detecting whether the inherent structure of two random instances from two models is equivalent, thus making the evaluation stable when altering instance parameters.

2.3 Model Isomorphism As an alternative, we further define model isomorphism to capture structural equivalence between models, rather than relying on numerical optimal values. We say two optimization models M1 and M2 are model isomorphic if the following conditions hold: • Shared parameter support: M1 and M2 share the same parameter support Θ; • Existence of permutation-invariant transformation: There exists a permutation mapping F1 over decision variables and F2 over constraints such that, for any θ ∈Θ, the transformed instance F1 ◦F2(M1(θ)) is exactly M2(θ). Note that model isomorphism is sufficient for mutual reducibility: if M1 and M2 are isomorphic, then M1 and M2 are mutually model-losslessreducible. Optimization Model Equivalence Previous work typically assesses instance-level equivalence by comparing the optimal values of P1 = M1(θ) and P2 = M2(θ) for specific θ, where both the model and data are coupled in the input prompt. Different from previous work, we aim to detect model-level equivalence and consider a model-data separated framework for evaluating autonomous modeling systems.

Why: Uncertain or partial support: Available evidence motivates this generated claim, but it cannot directly prove the full claim.

Therefore, evaluating optimization modeling requires an equivalence notion that is invariant to superficial re-indexing but sensitive to structural modeling errors. Existing OR modeling benchmarks commonly rely on solver-based evaluation: the generated and reference formulations are solved under a fixed data configuration, and their optimal objective values are compared (Tang et al., 2024; Huang et al., 2024; AhmadiTeshnizi et al., 2024; Yang et al., 2024b). Non-equivalent models can coincidentally share the same optimal value; infeasible instances make objective-value comparison uninformative; repeated solver calls can be costly for large or difficult mixed interger programming (MILPs).

14. Memory Influence Summary

This section explains the role of persistent SQLite memory in the current run. Memory can store prior papers, references, hypotheses, evidence records, rejected evidence, and verification outcomes. Retrieval does not automatically become evidence: previous memory may influence query planning, flag duplicate-risk, or warn about previously rejected material, but direct verification is reused only when the current claim, reference, evidence, and rule version match. If memory was retrieved but did not materially affect the run, the report says so explicitly.

Retrieved + materially alteredMemory changed query planning, duplicate-risk assessment, or direct verification reuse.
Retrieved + not usedMemory was available but treated as context only; this is acceptable and should be reported honestly.
Duplicate risk highA similar previous hypothesis exists; novelty should be interpreted cautiously.
Negative memoryPreviously rejected snippets or noisy candidates are warning signals, never scientific counter-evidence by themselves.

Negative Memory

These are previous rejected evidence snippets or failed candidates. 16. Warnings and Limitations

This section collects automatic warnings and limitations detected during the run. Warnings are not automatic failures, but they mark places where a human reviewer should be cautious: shallow evidence, few retrieved papers, all-YELLOW claim support, high novelty risk, weak memory influence, API fallback, noisy reference extraction, or generated claims that remain mostly inferred.

No warningsThe automated checks found no obvious limitation, but human review is still required.
Evidence warningsSupport may be shallow, abstract-only, or missing full-text provenance.
Retrieval warningsFew connected papers, API fallback, or noisy search means the map may be incomplete.
Hypothesis warningsThe final idea may be mostly inferred, low-scoring, or duplicate-risk.
  • All selected-hypothesis claim support results are YELLOW; the generated idea is mostly inferred and needs new experiments.
  • Some hypothesis components are model-inferred without direct evidence IDs; review the Hypothesis Quality Audit before treating them as conclusions.
  • Generated hypothesis support is mainly indirect, background, or absent.

Real API mode depends on public endpoints, available abstracts/full text, and external service coverage. Absence of retrieved close prior work is not proof of novelty.

Appendix B. Evidence and Reference Audit

This appendix expands evidence provenance and reference parsing details. It shows how the input seed was identified, how raw references were cleaned, every evidence record with source and location, resolved input references, verification snippets, and rejected evidence. Use it when checking whether an evidence number in the main report really corresponds to readable text from a traceable source.

Seed identity reliableDetected from filename, metadata, URL, OpenReview ID, or title match rather than arbitrary references.

9(E-d2c61e094261)full_text_pdfinput_pdfORGEval: Graph-Theoretic Evaluation of LLMs in Optimization Modelingpdf:abstract:p10.95To address these limitations, we propose ORGEval, a graph-theoretic evaluation framework for assessing LLMs’ capabilities in formulating linear and mixed-integer linear programs (MILPs). Building on this result, ORGEval combines the WL-test for bipartite graphs with an efficient SD verification procedure to provide provably correct equivalence evaluation. We further introduce Bench4Opt, a benchmark dataset that separates models from data, to validate ORGEval and benchmark state-of-the-art LLMs on optimization modeling.
10(E-e6f4e323fda2)full_text_pdfinput_pdfORGEval: Graph-Theoretic Evaluation of LLMs in Optimization Modelingpdf:abstract:p10.95While Large Language Models (LLMs) show promise in automating this process, evaluating their performance remains difficult due to the absence of robust metrics. To address these issues, we propose ORGEval, a graph-theoretic evaluation framework for assessing LLMs’ capabilities in formulating linear and mixed-integer linear programs. We identify and prove a sufficient condition, when the tested graphs are symmetric decomposable (SD), under which the Weisfeiler–Lehman (WL) test is guaranteed to correctly detect isomorphism. Experimental results show that our method can successfully detect model equivalence and produce 100% consistent results across random parameter configurations, while significantly outperforming solver-based methods in runtime, especially on difficult problems. Our results reveal that although optimization modeling remains challenging for all LLMs, DeepSeek-V3 and Claude-Opus-4 achieve the highest accuracies under direct prompting, outperforming even leading reasoning models.

We further introduce Bench4Opt, a benchmark dataset that separates models from data, to validate ORGEval and benchmark state-of-the-art LLMs on optimization modeling. Experimental results demonstrate that ORGEval reliably detects equivalence while significantly outperforming solver-based methods in runtime, particularly on computationally challenging instances.

15(E-2b19eaaa7239)full_text_pdfopenreviewCertified Evaluation for LLMs in Optimization Modeling: From Graph Isomorphism to Formulation Isomorphismpdf:abstract:p10.95We study this issue in automated optimization modeling, where LLMs translate natural-language descriptions and numerical data into solver-ready formulations. Existing solver-based evaluation only approximates modeling correctness: it offers no formulation-level guarantee, is uninformative for infeasible cases, and can be expensive on hard mixed-integer linear programming. To address these limitations, We propose ORGEval, a graph-theoretic evaluation framework that represents optimization formulations as bipartite graphs and reduces equivalence checking to graph isomorphism.
16(E-33178a252021)full_text_pdfopenreviewCertified Evaluation for LLMs in Optimization Modeling: From Graph Isomorphism to Formulation Isomorphismpdf:limitations:p20.95Finally, empirical results obtained through this benchmark reveal concrete capability limits and failure modes of current LLMs in optimization modeling. In our experiments, ORGEval achieves consistent structural evaluation across data configurations and is substantially faster than solver-based evaluation on difficult instances. When applied to state-of-the-art LLMs, Bench4Opt shows that automated optimization modeling remains challenging, and error analysis indicates that constraint specification and decision-variable design remain dominant failure modes. • We formalize optimization-modeling correctness as formulation isomorphism and reduce LP/MILP equivalence evaluation to graph isomorphism (Section 3.1 and Section 3.3).
17(E-665d34de8214)full_text_pdfopenreviewCertified Evaluation for LLMs in Optimization Modeling: From Graph Isomorphism to Formulation Isomorphismpdf:conclusion:p80.95All groundtruth instances in Bench4Opt are verified to satisfy the SD condition, making the benchmark a certified evaluation setting rather than only a collection of tasks and reference answers. Experiments show that ORGEval achieves 100% consistency across data configurations and evaluates hard MIPLIB instances within seconds, while solver-based evaluation can become unstable, uninformative, or computationally prohibitive. Benchmarking state-of-the-art LLMs on Bench4Opt reveals that optimization modeling remains difficult: the best models achieve only 54.82% pass@1 accuracy, and error analysis shows that constraint specification and decision-variable design are the dominant failure modes. ORGEval connects theory, benchmark design, and empirical analysis: graph isomorphism and SD provide a reliable evaluation oracle, Bench4Opt instantiates it with SD-certified examples, and the results expose current LLM limitations. This highlights a broader principle for structured-domain benchmarks: evaluation should specify not only model scores, but also when the oracle is provably reliable.
18(E-8364da586091)full_text_pdfopenreviewCertified Evaluation for LLMs in Optimization Modeling: From Graph Isomorphism to Formulation Isomorphismpdf:limitations:p20.95Since SD is preserved under isomorphism, any generated instance that is not SD can be safely rejected as non-equivalent; if it is SD, WL color comparison becomes a provably correct equivalence test. Thus, the benchmark contains not only tasks and labels, but also a checkable condition certifying when the evaluation oracle is reliable. Building on this theory, we introduce ORGEVAL, a graph-theoretic framework for evaluating LLM-generated LP/MILP formulations, and BENCH4OPT, a model-data separated benchmark for optimization modeling.
19(E-fb705a953fcf)full_text_pdfopenreviewCertified Evaluation for LLMs in Optimization Modeling: From Graph Isomorphism to Formulation Isomorphismpdf:abstract:p10.95Existing solver-based evaluation only approximates modeling correctness: it offers no formulation-level guarantee, is uninformative for infeasible cases, and can be expensive on hard mixed-integer linear programming. To address these limitations, We propose ORGEval, a graph-theoretic evaluation framework that represents optimization formulations as bipartite graphs and reduces equivalence checking to graph isomorphism. To make this practical and reliable, we introduce symmetric decomposability (SD), a checkable condition under which Weisfeiler-Lehman color equivalence is exactly equivalent to formulation isomorphism.

To make this practical and reliable, we introduce symmetric decomposability (SD), a checkable condition under which Weisfeiler-Lehman color equivalence is exactly equivalent to formulation isomorphism. We further constructed Bench4Opt, a benchmark that separates models from data and contains SD-certified ground-truth instances.

31(E-ccd7bf362f38)full_text_pdfinput_referenceOptimus: Scalable optimization modeling with (mi) lp solvers and large language modelspdf:background:p30.934(Yang et al., 2023) use LLMs to directly generate solutions to optimization problems without calling traditional solvers through prompt optimization to improve performance. 35(E-07a88d2d8e7f)full_text_pdfinput_referenceOrlm: Training large language models for optimization modelingpdf:results:p60.95Huang et al.: ORLM: A Customizable Framework in Training Large Models for Automated Optimization Modeling 6 To our best knowledge, this work is the first initiative to train open-source LLMs specifically for optimization modeling in real-world industries. The proposed approach significantly reduces the reliance on closed-source LLMs thus adding significant flexibility to the training process and greatly enhancing privacy preservation for industrial applications. This suggests that in the field of optimization modeling, our approach enables ORLMs with approximately 7 billion parameters to outperform many cutting-edge large language models, such as GPT-4, Llama-3.1, Qwen 2.5, and related agent-based frameworks.
36(E-47ec9c487fac)full_text_pdfinput_referenceOrlm: Training large language models for optimization modelingpdf:body:p10.95With the advent of large language models (LLMs), new opportunities have emerged to streamline and automate such task. This reliance stems from the scarcity of high-quality training datasets for optimization modeling, resulting in elevated costs, prolonged processing times, and privacy concerns. To address these challenges, our work is the first to propose a viable path for training open-source LLMs that are capable of optimization modeling and developing solver codes, eventually leading to a superior ability for automating optimization modeling and solving. Particularly, we design the OR-Instruct, a semi-automated data synthesis framework for optimization modeling that enables customizable enhancements for specific scenarios or model types. We train several 7B-scale open-source LLMs using synthesized data (dubbed ORLMs), which exhibit significantly enhanced optimization modeling capabilities, achieving competitive performance across the NL4OPT, MAMO, and IndustryOR benchmarks.
37(E-defec9686112)full_text_pdfinput_referenceOrlm: Training large language models for optimization modelingpdf:limitations:p330.95Concluding Remarks In this paper, we propose a new path for the training of open-source large language models (LLMs) specifically tailored for optimization modeling. We characterize four critical requirements essential for the training dataset of optimization modeling LLMs and develop OR-Instruct, a semi-automated framework for generating synthetic data that meets these specific needs. We utilize the OR-Instruct data to train open-source LLMs with approximately 7 billion parameters, which significantly enhances their optimization modeling capabilities, achieving competitive performance across all test datasets.
38(E-68f180add4ce)full_text_pdfinput_referenceOrlm: Training large language models for optimization modelingpdf:results:p60.95Finally, we note that almost all of our results, including certain samples from the training data set, industry-level benchmark for optimization modeling, and all the best-performing LLMs that have been fine-tuned, are made open source. We hope that our work can serve as a foundation for subsequent explorations, empowering future researchers to enhance the utility and efficiency of large language models in optimization modeling. Section 3 delineates four essential conditions required for the training data and outlines the implementation specifics of the semi-automated synthetic data framework for optimized modeling, OR-Instruct. Section 4 examines the impact of training open-source large models on various test sets and presents the experimental outcomes of customized enhancements in modeling capabilities.

The advent of large language models (LLMs) has exposed a new frontier in combinatorial optimization, introducing opportunities to automate problem formulation, while also presenting new challenges—chief among them, the need for reliable equivalence checking.

The earliest method used in the NL4OPT benchmark [30] for evaluating formulation equivalence is canonical accuracy, which looks at direct equivalence between declarations (e.g., objective, constraints) between a reference correct formulation and a generated formulation.

B Related work NLP for OR Modeling While substantial progress has been made in automatic modeling of general mathematical problems (Bobrow, 1964; Dellarosa, 1986; Sundaram & Khemani, 2015; Liu et al., 2025), there has been limited focus on applying these techniques specifically to operations research. Prior to the rise of LLMs, the NL4Opt competition (Ramamonjison et al., 2022b) explored the feasibility of learning-based natural language interfaces for optimization solvers. More recently, works leveraging LLMs, such as the Chain-of-Experts (CoE) (Xiao et al., 2023) and OptiMUS (AhmadiTeshnizi et al., 2024), introduced multi-agent cooperative systems to model and code complex OR problems automatically. Furthermore, Tang et al. (2024) fine-tuned open-source LLMs with approximately 7B parameters, achieving significant performance improvements over baseline models. These advancements underscore the immense potential of LLMs in optimization modeling. With the emergence of LLMs, there is an increasing need for benchmarks to understand their capability boundaries (Liu et al., 2024b; Zhou et al., 2024; Sawada et al., 2023). Several optimization modeling benchmarks have been proposed to evaluate LLMsuncertain0.62fine-tuned open-source LLMs with approximately 7B parameters, achieving significant performance improvements over baseline models. These advancements underscore the immense potential of LLMs in optimization modeling. With the emergence of LLMs, there is an increasing need for benchmarks to understand their capability boundaries (Liu et al., 2024b; Zhou et al., 2024; Sawada et al., 2023). Several optimization modeling benchmarks have been proposed to evaluate LLMsBest bibliographic score 0.62; status uncertain.
The Linear Programming Word Problem (LPWP) dataset (Ramamonjison et al., 2022a) includes multiple domains and comprises up to 1,001 LP problems. However, it primarily consists of elementary-level LP problems. The ComplexOR dataset (Xiao et al., 2023) was designed to feature more complicated OR problems, but its limited size and inclusion of numerical data within the textual description still constrain the level of complexity it can represent. IndustryOR (Tang et al., 2024), MAMO (Huang et al., 2024), and E-OPT (Yang et al., 2024a) strive to cover a broader range of OR problems through data synthesis and augmentation. The NLP4LP dataset (AhmadiTeshnizi et al., 2024) attempts to separate data from textual descriptions, yet the problem sizes remain small, and the descriptions are well structured with variables/constraints/objectives explicitly presented. In comparison to existing benchmarks, our work aims to provide a more comprehensive dataset and a more rigorous evaluation method, enabling a more precise assessment of LLM capabilities in optimization modeling. Modeling Equivalence Evaluation The earliest work to evaluate NLP for OR modeling performance is to calculate the canonical accuracy (Ramamonjison et al., 2022a). This accuracy counts for the declaration-level(e.g., objective or constraints) matching score between predicted and reference formulations. This method has severe limitations as it’s highly sensitive to superficial differences in formulation, such as variable naming or ordering. More recent benchmark works—including MAMO (Huang et al., 2024), IndustryOR (Tang et al., 2024), NLP4LP (AhmadiTeshnizi et al., 2024), and OptiBench (Yang et al., 2024b), and equivamap (Zhai et al., 2025)—relies on solvers to assess modeling quality. They execute the predicted numerical models and compare the resulting optimal values with reference optimal values to evaluate correctness. While solver-based approaches better align with the functional goals of optimization, they introduce new limitations. The evaluation becomes dependent on solver behavior, which is often unstable, especially when the focus is on model structure equivalence rather than model instance outcome equivalence. For instance, small changes in parameters can render a model infeasible or non-convex, causing solvers to fail or return suboptimal solutions. As a result, optimal value mismatches may stem not from modeling errors but from solver or numerical issues, thereby confounding the reliability of equivalence assessment. Broader Research on AI for OR Beyond model formulation, significant progress has been made in the field ofnot_found0.47Best bibliographic score 0.47; status not_found.
AI for Operations Research (AI for OR), particularly in parameter generation and solving optimization problems (Rajgopal, 2004). In parameter generation, AI techniques have been employed for better simulation of key parameters of optimization problems (Elmachtoub & Grigas, 2022; Maragno et al., 2023; Bergman et al., 2022). Similarly, our work leverages LLMs to generate necessary problem data through a program of thoughts (Chen et al., 2022a). On the optimization side, numerous studies have focused on leveraging AI models in automatic algorithm configuration (Ansótegui et al., 2009; Lindauer et al., 2022; Anastacio & Hoos, 2020), optimization algorithm selection (Wang et al., 2019; Chi et al., 2022), and heuristic algorithm design (Zeng et al., 2022; Talbi, 2009; Romera-Paredes et al., 2024). Specifically, a line of research has modeled MILP/LP problems as bipartitenot_found0.378Best bibliographic score 0.38; status not_found.
graphs and applied Graph Neural Networks (GNNs) to make decisions at various stages of their solution processes (Gasse et al., 2019; Zhou et al., 2020). These GNN-based methods have demonstrated efficacy in tasks such as variable selection and node branching, leading to significant improvements in solver performance. Inspired by this, we model optimization problems as bipartite graphs and formalize the evaluation paradigm based on the classical WL-test algorithm Leman & Weisfeiler (1968). C Equivalence Evaluation C.1 Model Equivalence Class Definition C.1 (Model Equivalence). We say C(P) is a model equivalence class of the MILP/LP problem instance P if ∀ˆP ∈C(P), ∃permutation matrices P1, P2 which shuffles the index of a vector or column index of a matrix s.t. ˆP can be written in the following form: min x ˆcT x, s.t. ˆAxˆ◦ˆb, ˆl ≤x ≤ˆu where ˆb = P2b, ˆC = P1C, ˆA = P2AP1, ˆ◦= P2◦, ˆl = P1l, u = P1u. ∀P2 ∈C(P1), we say P2 is model-equivalent to P1, denote as P1 ∼P2. C.2 Weighted Bipartite Graph for Representing MILP/LP A weighted bipartite graph for a MILP/LP instance is denoted by G = (V ∪W, E), with vertex set V ∪W divided into 2 groups V = {v1, · · · , vm} for constraints, and W = {w1, · · · , wn} for variables, E consisting of Eij = E(vi, wj), ∀i = 1, · · · , m, j = 1, · · · , n. To fully represent all information in a MILP/LP instance, we associate each vertex with features: • The constraint vertex vi ∈V is equipped with a feature vector HV with elements hV i = (bi, oi) ∈HV = R × {≤, ≥, =, <, >} • The variable vertex wj ∈W is equipped with a feature vector HW with elements hW j = (cj, τj) ∈HW = R × {R ∪−∞} × {R ∪∞} × {0, 1}. τj = 1 if j ∈Z and τj = 0 otherwise. The edge Eij ∈R connects vi ∈V and wj ∈W, Eij = Aij. There is no edge connecting vertices in the same vertex group. C.3 Connection between Model Equivalence and Graph Isomorphism To test whether 2 modeling instances were permutation equivalent, we can equivalently conduct isomorphism testing between their corresponding weighted bipartite graphs. Lemma C.1 establishes an equivalence between assessing modeling appropriateness and graph isomorphism testing. Definition C.2 (Graph Isomorphism). Consider 2 graphs G1 = (G1, HV 1 × HW 1 ) and G2 = (G2, HV 2 × HW 2 ) with Gi = (Vi ∪Wi, Ei)|1≤i≤2. We say G1 and G1 are isomorphic if there exists permutation matrix P1, P2such that: P1E1P T 2 = E2, P1HW 1 = HW 2 , P2HV 1 = HV 2 . If 2 graphs G1 and G1 are isomorphic, denote G1 g∼G2. Lemma C.1. ∀MILP/LP instances P1, P2 with corresponding bipartite graph G1, G1, we have P1 ∼P2 ⇐⇒G1 g∼G2. C.4 Proof of lemma C.1: We prove this lemma by proving 2 claims: Claim 1: G1 ∼G2 =⇒P1 ∼P2. Suppose G1 ∼G2. For bipartite graphs G1 and G2, nodes vi would only connect with some node wj if the j-th constraint involves decision variable xi. Therefore the adjacency matrix of Gk would be in the form A(k) adj =  AT k Ak  , ∀k = 1, 2. Now, by the assumption that G1 ∼G2, ∃permutation matrix P such that P = PV PW  , PA(1) adjP = A(2) adj, PT V HV 1 = PT HV 2 , PT W HW 1 = PT W HW 2 . Therefore, we have A(2) adj =  PV AT PW A1  and H2 =  PV HV PW HW  . We may reformulate the MILP/LP instance P2 as follows: P2 : min x∈Rp×{0,1}n−p cT PV x, s.t. Function checkDisjointness([Cluster[1], . . . , Cluster[k −1]]) outputs True if any two sets Cluster[i] and Cluster[j], where i ̸= j, share a common element. C.6 Proof Preparation for Theorem 3.1 Before establishing the proof, we first introduce the coloring refinement process of WL test for MILP/LP problem since it is the first step 1 in algorithm A. For iteration l of the algorithm we will be assigning to each node a tuple HL i containing the node’s old compressed label and a multiset of the node’s neighbors’ compressed labels. A multiset is a set (a collection of elements where order is not important) where elements may appear multiple times. At each iteration l, we will additionally be assigning to each node a new “compressed” label CL i with the same HL i will get the same compressed label. Repeat the above process for up to (m+n) (the number of nodes) iterations or until the partition of nodes by compressed label does not change from one iteration to the next, we will get a converged multiset. In addition, we introduce preliminary tools for an algorithm-independent definition. In fact, unfoldable and symmetric decomposable can be defined without relying on WL-test algorithm. We introduced equivalent definitions based on stable partition index sets. Definition C.3 (Stable Partition Index Sets). For a modeling instance P in the form of (1) with n decision variables and n constraints, define index set for optimization variables by I = {I1, I2, · · · , Is} and index set for constraints by J = {J1, J2, · · · , Jt}, where • Ss l=1 Il = {1, 2, · · · , m}, St k=1 Jk = {1, 2, · · · , n}; • Ili ∩Ilj = ∅, Jkp ∩Jkq = ∅, ∀i, j ∈[1, · · · , |Il|], i ̸= j, and p, q ∈[1, · · · , |Jk|], p ̸= q. We say (I, J ) is a pair of stable partition index sets if the following condition holds: 1. (ci, τi) = (ci′, τi′), ∀i, i′ ∈Ip for some p ∈1, 2, · · · , s; 2. (bj, ◦j) = (bj′, ◦j′), ∀j, j′ ∈Jq for some q ∈1, 2, · · · , t; 3. ∀p ∈1, 2, · · · , s, q ∈1, 2, · · · , t, and i, i′ ∈Ip, we have P j∈Jq aij = P j∈Jq ai′j; 4. ∀p ∈1, 2, · · · , s, q ∈1, 2, · · · , t, and j, j′ ∈Jq, we have P i∈Ip aij = P i∈Ip aij′; Lemma C.2. If there are no collision of hash functions and their weighted averages, then WL test algorithm 2 will finally terminated at some stable partition in O(m + n) iterations. Lemma C.2 is proved in Chen et al. (2022b). Definition C.4 (Unfoldable, by trivial partition). To address these limitations, We propose ORGEval, a graph-theoretic evaluation framework that represents optimization formulations as bipartite graphs and reduces equivalence checking to graph isomorphism. CL3 - YELLOW

While Large Language Models (LLMs) show promise in automating this process, evaluating their performance remains difficult due to the absence of robust metrics. To address these issues, we propose ORGEval, a graph-theoretic evaluation framework for assessing LLMs’ capabilities in formulating linear and mixed-integer linear programs. We identify and prove a sufficient condition, when the tested graphs are symmetric decomposable (SD), under which the Weisfeiler–Lehman (WL) test is guaranteed to correctly detect isomorphism. Experimental results show that our method can successfully detect model equivalence and produce 100% consistent results across random parameter configurations, while significantly outperforming solver-based methods in runtime, especially on difficult problems. Our results reveal that although optimization modeling remains challenging for all LLMs, DeepSeek-V3 and Claude-Opus-4 achieve the highest accuracies under direct prompting, outperforming even leading reasoning models.
Evidence ID: 52 (E-dbfbd7266e93); Source: input_pdf; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: ORGEval: Graph-Theoretic Evaluation of LLMs in Optimization Modeling; URL: D:\SciHypoCheck\reports\openclaw_inputs\openclaw_input_20260605_050047.pdf; Confidence: 0.95
To address these limitations, we propose ORGEval, a graph-theoretic evaluation framework for assessing LLMs’ capabilities in formulating linear and mixed-integer linear programs (MILPs). Building on this result, ORGEval combines the WL-test for bipartite graphs with an efficient SD verification procedure to provide provably correct equivalence evaluation. We further introduce Bench4Opt, a benchmark dataset that separates models from data, to validate ORGEval and benchmark state-of-the-art LLMs on optimization modeling.
Evidence ID: 53 (E-1887c0349dbf); Source: input_pdf; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: ORGEval: Graph-Theoretic Evaluation of LLMs in Optimization Modeling; URL: https://openreview.net/pdf/f996a8205c667a5941e8eed11013048be3c2fe4b.pdf; Confidence: 0.929
To make model-level equivalence evaluation tractable, we reduce model "equivalence" to model isomorphism, a stricter form of equivalence that focuses on the inherent structure of models rather than their optimal solutions. Importantly, we observe that if two instances are isomorphic, their isomorphism remains unchanged under different data configurations. ORGEval is developed to evaluate modeling equivalence by detecting whether the inherent structure of two random instances from two models is equivalent, thus making the evaluation stable when altering instance parameters.

By focusing on structural equivalence rather than instance-level configurations, ORGEval is robust to numerical variations.

Evidence ID: 56 (E-ee1ca7f1c555); Source: input_pdf; Location: abstract; Paper: ORGEval: Graph-Theoretic Evaluation of LLMs in Optimization Modeling; URL: https://arxiv.org/abs/0605.05004; Confidence: 0.919
2.3 Model Isomorphism As an alternative, we further define model isomorphism to capture structural equivalence between models, rather than relying on numerical optimal values. We say two optimization models M1 and M2 are model isomorphic if the following conditions hold: • Shared parameter support: M1 and M2 share the same parameter support Θ; • Existence of permutation-invariant transformation: There exists a permutation mapping F1 over decision variables and F2 over constraints such that, for any θ ∈Θ, the transformed instance F1 ◦F2(M1(θ)) is exactly M2(θ). Note that model isomorphism is sufficient for mutual reducibility: if M1 and M2 are isomorphic, then M1 and M2 are mutually model-losslessreducible. Optimization Model Equivalence Previous work typically assesses instance-level equivalence by comparing the optimal values of P1 = M1(θ) and P2 = M2(θ) for specific θ, where both the model and data are coupled in the input prompt. Different from previous work, we aim to detect model-level equivalence and consider a model-data separated framework for evaluating autonomous modeling systems.

Therefore, evaluating optimization modeling requires an equivalence notion that is invariant to superficial re-indexing but sensitive to structural modeling errors. Existing OR modeling benchmarks commonly rely on solver-based evaluation: the generated and reference formulations are solved under a fixed data configuration, and their optimal objective values are compared (Tang et al., 2024; Huang et al., 2024; AhmadiTeshnizi et al., 2024; Yang et al., 2024b). Atomic Claims

  • CL1: Tension X1: ORGEval claims 100% consistency across data configurations, but functional equivalence is the practical goal. If structural equivalence does not imply functional equivalence, ORGEval may miss practically important differences. (gap_motivation)
    Evidence IDs: 12(E-15b76c6ae8f3) 8(E-e15f916a77ba)
  • CL2: Directly challenges the practical relevance of ORGEval's metric; if true, would motivate hybrid evaluation combining structural and solver-based checks. (novelty_rationale)
    Evidence IDs: 12(E-15b76c6ae8f3) 8(E-e15f916a77ba)
  • CL3: ORGEval's structural equivalence does not correlate perfectly with functional equivalence (same optimal value) for MILP formulations; there exist structurally equivalent models that yield different optimal values under different data configurations. (novel_claim_requires_experiment)
    Evidence IDs: 12(E-15b76c6ae8f3) 8(E-e15f916a77ba)

Isomorphism As an alternative, we further define model isomorphism to capture structural equivalence between models, rather than relying on numerical optimal values. We say two optimization models M1 and M2 are model isomorphic if the following conditions hold: • Shared parameter support: M1 and M2 share the same parameter support Θ; • Existence of permutation-invariant transformation: There exists a permutation mapping F1 over decision variables and F2 over constraints such that, for any θ ∈Θ, the transformed instance F1 ◦F2(M1(θ)) is exactly M2(θ).
